NOAA 10 nonscanner passive analog temperatures.\nmean, and maximum\n.............................................................\n\nvii\n\nDaily values of\n\n405\nDaily values of minimum,\n406\n\nAbstract\nInstruments\nof the Earth Radiation\nBudget Experiment\n(ERBE) have operated on\nthree different\nEarth-orbiting\nspacecraft.\nThe Earth Radiation\nBudget\nSatellite\n(ERBS) is operated\nby the National Aeronautics\nand Space Administration\n(NASA),\nand the NOAA 9 and NOAA 10 weather satellites\nare operated by the National\nOceanic and Atmospheric\nAdministration\n(NOAA).\nThis paper is one of a series that\ndescribes\nthe ERBE mission,\nin-orbit\nenvironments,\ninstrument\ndesign and operational features,\nand data processing\nand validation\nprocedures.\nThis paper also\ndescribes\nthe in-flight\noperations\nfor the ERBE nonscanner\ninstruments\naboard the\nERBS, NOAA 9, and NOAA\n10 spacecraft\nfrom January\n1990 through\nDecember\n1990. Validation\nand archival of radiation measurements\nmade by ERBE nonscanner\ninstruments\nduring this period\nwere completed\nin August\n1996. This paper covers\nnormal and special operations\nof the spacecraft\nand instruments,\noperational\nanomalies, and the responses\nof the instruments\nto in-orbit and seasonal variations\nin the\nsolar environment.\nIntroduction\n\nment\n\nThe objective of the Earth Radiation\nBudget Experi(ERBE)\nis to determine\nlong-term\ntrends\nin\n\nmonthly averages of the Earth\'s longwave\nand shortwave\nradiation\nfields.\nTo accomplish\nthis objective,\nERBE\ninstruments\nwere launched\ninto Earth orbits aboard the\nEarth Radiation\nBudget\nSatellite\n(ERBS)\n(operated\nby\nGoddard\nSpace Flight Center (GSFC))\nin October\n1984\nand aboard the NOAA 9 and NOAA 10 spacecraft\n(operated by the National\nOceanic and Atmospheric\nAdministration\n(NOAA))\nin December\n1984 and September\n1986, respectively.\nValidation\nand archival of data from\nthe first 15 months of instrument\noperation,\nNovember\n1984 through January\n1986, were completed\nin March\n\nThis paper describes\nthe in-orbit\noperation\nof the\nERBE\nnonscanner\ninstrument\naboard\nthe\nERBS,\nNOAA 9, and NOAA\n10 spacecraft\nfrom\nJanuary\nthrough December\n1990. Validation\nand archival of data\nfrom these satellite\nmonths\nwere completed\nin August\n1996. The discussion\nincludes\nnormal and special spacecraft and instrument\noperations,\noperational\nanomalies,\nand the response\nof the nonscanner\ninstruments\nto inorbit and seasonal variations in the solar environment.\nNomenclature\n\nAcronyms\n\nand Abbreviations\n\nACR\n\nactive cavity\n\nBB\n\nblackbody\n\nNOAA spacecraft\nduring that 15-month\nperiod. Reference 1 also gives an overview\nof the ERBE mission, the\ndesign and operational\nfeatures of the ERBE instruments,\nand a description\nof the ERBE science data processing.\nValidation\nand archival of data from the second year of\n\nCAL\n\ncalibration\n\nCDC/NOS\n\nControl Data Corporation/Network\nOperating\nSystem\n\nCPU\n\ncentral\n\ninstrument\noperations,\nFebruary\n1986 through\nJanuary\n1987,\nwere\ncompleted\nin July\n1991.\nReference\n2\ndescribes\nthe operation\nof the ERBE instruments\nduring\nthat second year. Reference\n3 describes\nthe in-orbit oper-\n\nDAAC\n\nDistributed\n\nEOSDIS\n\nEarth Observing\nSystem\nInformation\nSystem\n\nation of the ERBE instruments\naboard the ERBS spacecraft from February\n1987 through February\n1990 and of\nthe instruments\naboard\nthe NOAA\n10 spacecraft\nfrom\n\nERBE\n\nEarth Radiation\n\nBudget\n\nExperiment\n\nERBS\n\nEarth\n\nBudget\n\nSatellite\n\nFebruary\n1987 through May 1989. Validation\nand archival of data from these satellite months were completed\nin\nMay 1992. KZ\n\ncoordinate\n\n(X\n\nazimuth\n\ngenerates\n\naxes\n\nThe Information\nProcessing\nand processes\nspacecraft\nand\n\nERBE\n\ndata for LaRC.\n\nof orbit angular\n\nvelocity\n\nvector\n\ndeg\n\nbeta angle (angle between Sun and orbit\nangular momentum\nvectors), deg\n(scan)\n\nNOAA 9 and NOAA 10 operational\nmeteorological\nsatellites in December\n1984 and September\n1986, respectively.\nThe Payload\nOperations\nand Control\nCenter\n(POCC) at GSFC directs operations\nof the ERBS spacecraft and its ERBE and Stratospheric\nAerosol\nand Gas\nExperiment\n(SAGE) II instruments\nby using both ground\nstations\nand the Tracking\nand Data Relay\nSatellite\n\ntelemetry\ndata from ERBS and provides\nthat data to\nLangley Research\nCenter (LaRC) for further processing.\nGSFC also provides\nLaRC with ephemeris\ndata for all\nthree spacecraft.\nThe Satellite\nOperations\nand Control\nCenter (SOCC) at the National\nEnvironmental\nSatellite,\nData, and Information\nService\n(NESDIS)\noperates\nthe\nNOAA\nspacecraft\nand their ERBE instruments,\nprovides\ndecommutation\nprocessing\nof the telemetry\ndata, and\n\ntime\n\nangle,\n\nThe ERBS spacecraft,\nlaunched\nby the Space Shuttle\nChallenger\nin October\n1984, was the first spacecraft\nto\ncarry ERBE instruments\ninto orbit. The second and third\nsets of ERBE\ninstruments\nwere launched\naboard the\n\nSystem (TDRSS)\nnetwork.\nDivision at GSFC receives\n\nunit vector\nmomentum\n\nelevation\n\nto-global\nobtained\n\nsensor\n\nSymbols\nazimuth\n\nThe goal of the Earth Radiation\nBudget Experiment\n(ERBE) is to produce monthly averages of Iongwave and\nshortwave\nradiation parameters\non the Earth at regional-\n\nflying on three separate\nspacecraft.\nThese three spacecraft are the Earth Radiation\nBudget\nSatellite\n(ERBS)\n\nS-7\n\nA,B\n\nangle\n\nangle, deg\n\nData Processing,\nValidation,\nScience Data Products\n\nand Distribution\n\nof\n\nLangley Research\nCcntcr has the responsibility\nof\nprocessing and validating all science data from the ERBE\nmission and of distributing the resulting data products to\nthe science\ncommunity.\nThe ERBE\ndata processing\nsystem at LaRC uses modular\nsoftwarc\nsubsystems\nto\nprocess the ERBE data, starting with the input telemetry\n\n2\n\niq\n\nl!\n\nand ephemeris\ndata from GSFC and NOAA, and ending\nwith the production\nof the required science data products.\nFigure\nprocessing,\n\n1 shows\ntogether\n\nthe major steps in the science data\nwith the primary input and output\n\nproducts.\nDetails of the data processing\nare provided\nin\nreference\n1. Some changes\nto the data processing,\nas\ndescribed\nin reference\n1, have been implemented\nto handle processing\nof only nonscanner\ndata; data processing\nchanges\nhave also been implemented\nbecause\nof the\nchange in the data processing\nplatform from a CDC/NOS\noperating\nsystem to a Unix operating\nsystem. In the current system, as illustrated\nin figure 1, 24-hour telemetry\ndata files from ERBS, NOAA 9, or NOAA 10 spacecraft\nare transferred\nelectronically\nto the Langley\nDistributed\nActive\nArchive\nCenter (DAAC),\nwhich is part of the\nEarth Observing\nSystem Data and Information\nSystem\n(EOSDIS).\nAlso, 8-day ephemeris\ndata sets from GSFC\nfor all three spacecraft\nare transferred\nelectronically\nto\nthe DAAC. Although\nthe word tape is used in many of\nthe data product names, tapes are no longer used in the\nERBE data processing\nsystem, and all data products exist\nas electronic\nfiles. The ephemeris\ndata are processed,\nseparated\ninto single-day\nfiles, and input, along with the\nraw telemetry data, into the daily and top of atmosphere\n(TOA) processing\nsystem. Major data products from this\nsystem are the instrument\nvalidation\ntape (IVT), which\ngoes to the LaRC Science\nTeam for validation\nof the\ninstrument\ncalibration,\nand the medium-wide\ndata tape\n(S-7), which is archived\nat the LaRC DAAC.\nThe S-7\nproduct is the nonscanner-only\nversion of the Processed\nArchival\nTape (PAT) product described\nin reference\n1,\nwhich included\nboth scanner and nonscanner\ndata. The\nquality control (QC) reports generated\nby the daily and\nTOA processing\nsystem are sent to the ERBE data analysts, and if approved,\nthe radiant fluxes at TOA that are\ncalculated\nfrom this first step in the processing\nare input\ninto the next processing\nstep, the monthly\ntime-space\naveraging\nsystem. The QC reports and validation\ngraphics from this processing\nstep are sent to the ERBE data\nanalysts\nand the LaRC Science\nTeam, respectively.\nIf\napproved,\nthe monthly\naverages\n(S-10N) and the nested\naverages\n(S-4N and S-4GN)\nare archived\nat the LaRC\nDAAC.\ndirected\n\nRequests\nfor ERBE data\nto the LaRC DAAC 1.\n\nproducts\n\nshould\n\nbe\n\nTable\n1 presents\nsummary\ninformation\nabout the\ninternal\ndata product\none for nonscanner\ninstrument\n(ID1N) and the S-7 archival product for each spacecraft\nfor each month of operation\ncovered in this paper. The ERBE nonscanner\n(fig. 2) has\nseveral important\nfeatures. The instrument\nhas rotating\nazimuth and elevation beams that have the capability\nto\nrotate the optical axes of the detectors\nin two degrees of\nfreedom. The instrument\ncan perform two different\ntypes\nof in-flight calibrations:\nsolar calibrations\nusing the Sun\nas the calibration\nsource, and internal calibrations\nusing\ntemperature-controlled\nblackbodies\n(BB)\nand special\nShortwave\nInternal\nCalibration\nSources (SWlCS).\nThe\ninstrument\nhas microprocessors\nthat process and execute\nground-commanded\nor stored commands\nto direct and\ncontrol operations.\nThe nonscanner\ninstrument\n(fig. 2) consists of four\nEarth-viewing\ndetectors\nand one solar monitor detector\nlocated on the head assembly.\nThe four Earth-viewing\ndetectors\nare unchopped\nactive\ncavity\nradiometers\n(ACR\'s),\nwhereas\nthe solar monitor\nis an unfiltered\nchopped ACR designed to measure direct solar radiation\nfor calibrating\nthe Earth-viewing\ndetectors.\nTwo of these\ndetectors have wide field-of-view\n(WFOV) apertures that\nallow the detectors\nto view the entire disk of the Earth;\nthe other two detectors\nhave medium\nfield-of-view\n(MFOV)\napertures\nthat allow the\narea about\n!100 km in diameter.\nviewing detectors,\none WFOV\nsolar monitor detector\nmeasure\n\ndetectors\nto view an\nTwo of the Earth-\n\nand one MFOV,\ntotal radiation,\n\nand the\nwhereas\n\nthe other two Earth-viewing\ndetectors\nmeasure\nshortwave radiation.\nThe spectral\ncharacteristics\nof the five\nnonscanner\ndetectors are listed in table 2. The total radiation detectors\nare unfiltered,\nand the shortwave\nspectral\nbands are achieved\nby use of fused silica dome filters\nplaced over the detectors.\nThe\ndifferent\n\nnonscanner\ninstrument\nmodes so that radiation\n\ncan operate\nin several\nmeasurements\ncan be\n\nmade over a wide range of operational\nconditions.\nThe\ninstrument\nhas a microprocessor\nto control and direct the\nvarious operations.\nTable 3 lists the operational\nand pulse\ndiscrete commands,\nwhich are discussed\nin detail in reference 1. The instrument\ncan operate at fixed elevationbeam positions of 0 \xc2\xb0 (nadir), 78 \xc2\xb0 (solar ports), and ! 80 \xc2\xb0\n(stow or internal calibration\nposition),\nand at azimuth\nangles between 0 \xc2\xb0 and 180 \xc2\xb0.\nThe ERBE nonscanner\ninstrument\noutput consists of\na complete\ncycle of radiometric\nand housekeeping\nmeasurements\nevery 16 seconds. Pertinent coordinate systems and in-flight geometry are described here, beginning\nwith a description of\nthe instrument coordinate axes. An additional description\nof the general Earth-Sun-spacecraft\nappendix B of reference\nI.\n\ngeometry\n\nis given in\n\nWhen discussing detector pointing vectors, it is convenient to assume that the origin of a set of coordinate\naxes is at the focal point of the detector of interest. Figure 2 illustrates the fixed and rotating axes systems of the\nnonscanner instrument. The fixed axes of the nonscanner\ninstrument are noted by the subscript NS. The axes of the\nrotating azimuth beam are noted by the subscript _, and\nthe axes of the rotating elevation beam are noted by the\nsubscript\nThe\n\n_.\nazimuth\n\nbeam\n\nof each\n\ninstrument\n\nhas a single\n\ndegree of freedom relative to the fixed axes, permitting\nthe entire head assembly\n(structure below pedestal) to\nrotate about the fixed X-axis. The rotating s-axes\nare\naligned with the fixed axes when the rotation angle o_ is\nzero. A positive\nrotation (clockwise)\nabout the fixed\nX-axis of either instrument produces a positive azimuth\nangle oq which is measured from the fixed Z-axis. The\nazimuth beam of either instrument can rotate between\nangles of 0 \xc2\xb0 and 180 \xc2\xb0.\nThe\n\nnonscanner\n\nelevation\n\nbeam\n\ncan\n\nrotate in one\n\ndegree of freedom relative to the azimuth beam, permitting the optical axes of the four Earth-viewing\ndetectors\nto rotate about the Yet-axis. Figure 2 shows the alignment\nof the rotating @axes with the fixed axes and rotating\ncx-axes of the nonscanner instrument when the elevation\nangle _ is zero. A negative (counterclockwise)\nrotation\nabout the rotating\nYccaxis of the nonscanner instrument\nproduces\na positive elevation angle _, which is measured\nfrom the fixed X-axis. The elevation beam operates only\nat three elevation\npositions:\n0 \xc2\xb0 (nadir), 78 \xc2\xb0 (solar ports),\nand 180 \xc2\xb0 (internal calibration\nsource, or stow). The optical axis of the solar monitor is fixed on the azimuth beam\n\nat an elevation angle of 78 \xc2\xb0 , which\nspacecraft horizon.\n\nis 12 \xc2\xb0 down\n\nfrom the\n\nFigure 3 illustrates\nhow the fixed axes of the ERBE\ninstruments\nare aligned with the axes of the spacecraft\non\nwhich they are mounted.\nThe ERBS spacecraft\naxes have\nthe subscript\nnotation E (ERBS),\nand NOAA spacecraft\naxes have the subscript\nnotation\nN (NOAA).\nNOAA 9\nand NOAA 10 have the same coordinate\nsystem. As in\nfigure 2, NS refers to the nonscanner\ninstrument.\nNote\nthat only the orientation\nof these axes systems relative to\neach other are important,\nnot the locations\nof their origins. The positive Y-axis of the ERBS spacecraft\nis in the\ndirection in which the solar panels are tilted, and the positive Z-axis of each NOAA spacecraft\nis parallel to the\naxis of the boom which supports\nthe spacecraft\nsolar\npanel.\nFigure\n\n4 illustrates\n\nhow the axes of the two types\n\nof\n\nspacecraft are aligned with their respective\nin-flight local\nhorizon axes, and on which side of the orbit the Sun is\npositioned\nrelative\nto the orbit plane\nand spacecraft\nvelocity vector. Here VLH is the component\nof the spacecraft velocity vector in the local horizon plane, lq is the\norbit angular momentum\nvector,\ncate the direction\nof local nadir\n\nand XLH and ZLr I indifor NOAA\nand ERBS\n\nspacecraft,\nrespectively.\nFigure 4 also shows the position\nof the instrument\nazimuth beam (or-axes system) relative\nto the local horizon\nsystem when the rotating\nazimuth\naxes are aligned\n\nwith the fixed axes.\n\nThe attitude\nor orientation\nangles of a spacecraft,\nwhich are provided\nin the telemetry\ndata, are defined\nrelative to the specific local horizon system in which the\nspacecraft\noperates.\nThe spacecraft\nattitude\nangles and\nthe azimuth\nand elevation\nangles of the instruments\nare\nused to compute\nthe pointing\nvectors\nof the primary\n(Earth-viewing)\nradiometric\ndetectors,\nas well as those of\nthe solar monitor, in the appropriate\nlocal horizon system\nof figure 4. The pointing vectors for the ERBS spacecraft\nof figure 4(a) are then transformed\ninto the NOAA local\nhorizon system of figure 4(b) so that all pointing vectors\nhave a common\nlocal horizon\nsystem.\nThe pointing\nvectors in this common\naxes system are used to compute\nthe\nEarth\nlocations\nof\nthe\nprimary\nradiometric\nmeasurements.\nWhen the ERBS spacecraft\nis flying X-axis forward\n(positive X-axis in the direction of the positive spacecraft\nvelocity vector), the Sun is normally\non the right side of\nthe ERBS orbit (looking downrange\nor down the velocity\nvector).\nWhen the Sun crosses the ERBS orbit plane\nfrom right to left, the spacecraft\nis yawed (rotated about\nnadir or Z,_axis)\n180 \xc2\xb0 to reposition\nthe solar panels so\nthat they tilt to the left side of the orbit. About 36 days\nlater, when the Sun again crosses the orbit plane, this\ntime from left to right, the spacecraft\nis again rotated\n\n!\'1 Iiii\n\n180 \xc2\xb0. The NOAA spacecraft\nare in approximate\nSunsynchronous\norbits, and they always fly with their Y-axes\nalong the negative\nvelocity\nvector with the Sun on the\nleft side of the orbit.\nAppendix\n\nB\n\nin\n\nreference\n\n1 describes\n\nthe\n\nlocal\n\nhorizon coordinate\nsystem in which the Sun position is\nnormally calculated.\nThe azimuth and elevation angles of\nthe Sun in this system can be directly related to the Sun\nangles in the instrument\naxes systems of the ERBE nonscanner instrument\ndescribed earlier in this section.\nGeneral\nand\n\nDiscussion\n\nInstrument\nThis section\n\nand\n\nAnalysis\n\nof Mission\n\nOperations\npresents\n\na discussion\n\nof the instruments\n\naboard\neach spacecraft\nseparately,\nbeginning\nbrief description\nof operational\nresponsibilities\nprocedures.\nAn overview\nof calibrations\nand\n\nwith a\nand\nnormal\n\nEarth-viewing\noperations\nis then presented,\nfollowed\ndiscussions\nof the effects of the solar environment\ninstrument\ninstrument\nERBS\nThe\n\noperations,\nhousekeeping\n\nof operational\nanomalies,\nmeasurements.\n\nby\non\n\nand of\n\nSpacecraft\nERBS\n\nspacecraft\n\nand\n\nthe\n\nERBE\n\nnonscanner\n\ninstrument\naboard\nit are controlled\nand operated\nby\nNASA\nat its Payload\nOperations\nand Control\nCenter\n(POCC)\nat Goddard\nSpace Flight Center,\nGreenbelt,\nMaryland.\nThe LaRC ERBE personnel\nare responsible\nfor planning\nchanges\nin instrument\noperation,\nand the\nplans are coordinated\nwith POCC personnel,\nwho implement the changes.\nThe operational\nstatus of the instruments and housekeeping\nmeasurements\nare monitored\ndirectly at the ERBS POCC during real-time\ntelecommunication\nlink between\nLaRC and\n\npasses. Because\nthe\nSun terminator\nis continuously\nin the limb-to-limb\nview\nof the Earth during these periods, the nonscanner\nWFOV\ndetectors\ndo not view any regions of the Earth which are\ntotally illuminated\nor totally dark.\nMonitoring\nand analysis of instrument\nhousekeepingmeasurements.\nInstrument\nhousekeeping\nmeasurements are monitored\nduring\nreal-time\ncommunication\ncontacts with the spacecraft\nto ensure that the instrument\nis functioning\nnormally.\nBecause\nthe ERBS spacecraft\norbit produces\na wide range of 13angles, which causes the\nERBE instrument\nonboard to experience\nlarge variations\nin heating\n\nand\n\nrequires\n\nchanges\n\nin normal\n\noperational\n\nmodes,\nmonitoring\nthe housekeeping\nmeasurements\nof\nthis instrument\nis particularly\nimportant.\nIn the real-time\nmonitoring\nprocedure,\nthe housekeeping\nmeasurements\nare checked\nagainst both the yellow limits, which indicate that an instrument\nmay be approaching\na critical\ncondition,\nand the red limits, which indicate\nthat the\ninstrument\nis at risk of being damaged.\nAn analysis\nof instrument\nhousekeeping\nmeasurements has also been performed\nduring the ERBE science\ndata processing.\nThis processing\nproduces\na complete\nhistory of the actual measured\nvalues of all housekeeping\ntemperatures\nand voltages,\nand it accumulates\nthe minimum, mean, and maximum\nvalues of all housekeeping\nmeasurements\nfor each archived\nday. The processing\nincludes\ntesting the value of every housekeeping\nmeasurement\nto determine\nif the value is within specified\nlimits and if its rate of change is less than a specified\nvalue.\nValues\nused to test the magnitudes\nand rate\nchanges\nof selected housekeeping\ninstrument\non the ERBS spacecraft\nThese edit limits are significantly\nthose used in the real-time monitoring\nabove. An additional\ndescription\nof the double maxima appearance\nis given in\nreference\nI.\nThe heat sink, aperture,\nand field-of-view\nlimiter\ntemperatures\nof the nonscanner\ninstrument\nall affect the\nradiometric\noutput of the Earth-viewing\ndetectors.\nThe\nheat sink temperatures\nof the Earth-viewing\ndetectors\nare\ntightly controlled,\nand the aperture\ntemperatures\nof the\nEarth-viewing\ndetectors\nare closely coupled to the heat\nsink temperatures;\ntherefore,\nthe effects of these temperatures\nare not\nmodeled\nin the\nradiometric\ndataconversion\nalgorithms.\nThe field-of-view\nlimiter temperatures of the nonscanner\ninstrument\nare not controlled,\nbut their values are accurately\nmeasured and are included\nin the radiometric\ndata-conversion\nalgorithms.\nWhen values of any of these measurements\nare flagged because\nthey fail the edit limit tests, the corresponding\nradiomettic data are rejected from further science data processing.\nThe heat sink temperatures\nvaried only about 0. l\xc2\xb0C\nduring the period covered by this paper, except for a few\ndays in February\nand one day in December\n(fig. 9). During these periods, when 13= 156 \xc2\xb0, the spacecraft\nwas in a\nnear full-sun orbit. Aperture temperatures\nvaried by less\nthan 0.6\xc2\xb0C, with peaks occurring\nduring periods of minimum or maximum\n_ angles (fig. 10). Because the fieldof-view\nlimiter\ntemperatures\nare not controlled,\nthey\nshow variations\nin temperature\nthat are sensitive\nto 13\n(fig. 11). The maximum\nvalues occur when\n13= 24 \xc2\xb0 or\n13-- 156 \xc2\xb0, when the Sun is very near the limb of the Earth.\nTemperatures\nof the solar monitor\nheat sink and\naperture (fig. 12) are not controlled,\nand their values are\nmore variable than those of the Earth-viewing\ndetectors.\nTherefore,\nthe effects of the variation of the solar monitor\n\ntemperatures\n\nare\n\nmodeled\n\nin the\n\nradiometric\n\ndata-\n\nconversion\nalgorithms\nduring\nprocessing\nof the data\nacquired during solar calibrations.\nHowever,\nbecause of\nthe extreme heating conditions,\ncalibrations\nare not performed during these full-Sun periods.\nAlso, during the\nFebruary,\nAugust, and December\nfull-Sun\nperiods,\nall\nsolar monitor heat sink and aperture temperatures\nfailed\ntelemetry edit limits. This failure is seen in the figure 12\nplots as steep-sided\ntroughs because the edited measurements register zero when all values are flagged as bad.\nThe nonscanner\nblackbodies\nare used primarily\nduring internal calibrations\nof the instruments,\nand variations in their temperatures\ndo not affect the output of the\nradiometric\ndetectors\nduring normal operation\n(fig. 13).\n\nThe spikes seen on the blackbody\ntions made when the blackbodies\n\nplots indicate\nwere turned\n\ncalibraon. The\n\nnonscanner\nelectronic\nslice 3 and power converter\nperatures\n(fig. 14) are used primarily\nin the real-time\n\ntemdata\n\nmonitoring\nprocedures.\nThe power converter\ntemperature\nis classified\nas a passive measurement\nbecause it is available in the telemetry data stream even if the ERBE nonscanner instrument\nis powered off. During most of this time, the instruments were\nin their\nnormal\noperating\nmodes.\nThe\ndiscussion\naddresses percentage\nof archived data (table 1), ]] angles\n(figs. 5 through 8), spacecraft\nmaneuvers (tables 1, 6, 7,\nand 8), instrument calibrations (tables 1, 6, 7, and 8), and\nother instrument operations (tables 1, 6, 7, and 8).\nTable 1 summarizes\nspacecraft\nand instrument operations for each spacecraft\nfor each month and also gives\nthe percentage of data for both the IDIN and S-7 products. The percentage\nof archived data for the S-7 product\nis actually the percentage\nof 16-second records that are\narchived. An archived record can contain fill and/or poor\nquality data that are flagged as bad. However,\nthe percentage of archived data is usually a good approximation\nof the percentage\nof usable\nfrom the ERBS spacecraft.\n\ndata, particularly\n\nfor data\n\nDifferences\nbetween the IDIN and S-7 data percentages arise because of data quality problems\nand because\nof constraints imposed on the data archived to the S-7.\nThese constraints\nrequire that (1) the instrument power\nbe on, (2) the instrument be in the Earth-viewing\nelevation mode, and (3) the instrument not be in solar or internal calibration mode. Data recovery was nearly always\ngreater from the ERBS spacecraft\nthan from the NOAA\nspacecraft. The losses in data recovery,\nas well as the\nlarger differences\nbetween\nthe NOAA\n9 and the\nNOAA 10 IDIN and S-7 data percentages,\nas compared\nto the ERBS data percentages,\noccur because of less efficient data processing\nprocedures\nat NOAA. These less\nefficient\nprocedures\nat NOAA\nreject\nthat the NOAA\nspacecraft\nare operational\nweather\nsatellites,\nwhereas\nERBS\nis dedicated\nto the ERBE\nand\nSAGE\nII\ninstruments.\nData quality problems\nare rarely\nERBS data, as is reflected in the small\nally less than 3 percent, between the\nwritten to the ERBS ID1N and S-7\n\n10\n\nYl |I\'.\n\nencountered\nin the\ndifferences,\ngenerpercentages\nof data\nproducts.\nOn days\n\nwhen ERBS spacecraft\nyaw maneuvers\nare performed,\ndifferences\nare generally\nabout 4 percent because some\ndata collected\nduring these maneuvers\ndo not meet the\nconstraints\nlisted above. On calibration\ndays, differences\nare generally\non the order of 22 percent for the ERBS\ndata and 30 to 35 percent for the NOAA data because\nmost of the data collected\nduring the calibrations\ndo not\nmeet the constraints\nidentified\nabove.\nThe combined\n\nfor each month covered\nin this discussion.\nFigures\n9\nthrough 26 show the responses\nof instrument\nhousekeeping temperatures\nand voltages to the operations\ndiscussed\nin this section, as well as the effects of changes in EarthSun spacecraft\ngeometry.\n\ninternal and solar calibration\nsequences\nperformed\non the\nnonscanner\ninstruments\non the NOAA spacecraft\nin 1990\nlasted approximately\n10 hours, with the instrument\nnot in\nthe Earth-viewing\nelevation mode for approximately\n8 of\nthose hours, or for about 32 percent of the day. The calibration\nsequence\nimplemented\non the ERBS spacecraft\nnonscanner\ninstrument\nwas shorter,\nlasting\napproximately\n7 hours, with the instrument\nnot in the Earthviewing\nelevation\nmode for approximately\n5 hours, or\nabout 20 percent of the day. The calibration\nsequences\ndiffered in length between the ERBS and NOAA spacecraft because\nof timing\ndifferences\nin the command\nuploading\nprocedures.\nAlso, the azimuth\nangle uploads\nwere executed prior to the calibrations\nperformed\non the\nERBS nonscanner\ninstrument,\nand during\nthe calibrations performed on the NOAA nonscanner\ninstruments.\n\nERBS spacecraft--January\n1990. In January\n1990\nthe percentage\nof data written to the IDIN was 99.99 and\nthe percentage\nof data archived\nto the S-7 was 97.73.\n(See table 1 (a).) The [3 angle decreased\nfrom about 77 \xc2\xb0 at\nthe beginning\nof the month to about 55 \xc2\xb0 on January\n12,\nand then increased\nto about\n104 \xc2\xb0 by January\n31. (See\nfigs. 5 and 6.) The spacecraft\nwas configured\nwith its\nX-axis negative\nfrom the beginning\nof the month until\n18:50 UTon January 25 when a 180 \xc2\xb0 yaw maneuver\nwas\nperformed.\nThe spacecraft\noperated\nwith its X-axis positive for the remainder\nof the month.\n\nThe data percentages\nlisted in the corresponding\ntables in references\n1, 2, and 3 show a much smaller difference\nbetween\nthe percentages\narchived\nto the raw\narchival\ntape (RAT) and the processed\narchival\ntape\n(PAT) products\nbecause\neach record in these products\nincludes both scanner and nonscanner\ndata, and a record\nwas written to the PAT product as long as it contained at\nleast one good scanner or nonscanner\nmeasurement.\nThe\nscanner\ncalibrations\nwere much shorter,\napproximately\none hour for the combined\ninternal-solar\ncalibration,\nthan\nwere the nonscanner\ncalibrations.\nA record\nthat conrained nonscanner\ncalibration\ndata along with scanner\nEarth-viewing\ndata would have been written to the PAT\nproduct but not to the S-7 product, so that the PAT product contained\nmore data records than the corresponding\nS-7 product.\nAll operational\nmode commands\nexecuted\nby the\nnonscanner\ninstruments\non the ERBS, the NOAA 9, and\nthe NOAA\n10 spacecraft\nfrom January\n1990 through\nDecember\n1990 are listed in tables 6, 7 and 8, respectively.\nThese tables are based on the command\necho\nword from the telemetry\ndata processing,\nwhich is an\necho of the last command\nexecuted\nby the instrument.\nOccasionally,\na data dropout\nwill obscure\na command\nthat was actually received\nand executed\nby the instrument so that the commands\nlisted in tables 6 through 8\nmay not exactly reflect instrument\noperations.\nSuch discrepancies\nare noted in the text and tables.\nFigure 5\nshows the 13 angles for ERBS, NOAA 9, and NOAA 10\nfor the entire year. Operations\nof the ERBE scanner instruments are discussed in references 1 through 3. No ERBE scanner data are available\nafter February 28, 1990, when the last operational ERBE\nscanner instrument,\nthat aboard the ERBS spacecraft,\nfailed. This publication\naddresses only the ERBE nonscanner instruments.\nThe NOAA 10 spacecraft was deactivated by NOAA\non November\n15, 1994, so no ERBE data are available\nfrom this spacecraft\nafter that date. The ERBE nonscanner instrument\naboard the NOAA\n9 spacecraft\nwas\n\npowered by NOAA on April 7, 1997. he ERBE\noff\nT\nnonscanner\ninstrument\naboard\ntheERBS\nspacecraft\ncontinues operate and to provide valuable scientific data.\nto\nData\n\nCoverage\n\nand Archival\n\nData coverage for the ERBE nonscanner instruments\non the ERBS and National\nOceanic\nand Atmospheric\nAdministration\n(NOAA)\nspacecraft\nspans all of 1990.\nArchival\nto the Distributed\nActive\nArchive\nCenter\n(DAAC)\ncompleted\n\nfor the period of these\nin August 1996.\n\nnonscanner\n\ndata\n\nwas\n\nThe monthly\naverage\nrate of ERBE data from the\nnonscanner\ninstrument\naboard the ERBS spacecraft\nwritten to the internal data product one nonscanner\n(IDIN)\nwas nearly 100 percent, while the monthly average archival rate for the medium wide data tape (S-7) product was\nnearly 98 percent. There were more data losses, and the\ndata coverage\nwas somewhat\nmore variable,\nfor the\nERBE instruments\naboard the NOAA 9 and NOAA\nI0\nspacecraft.\nThe monthly\naverage\nrate of NOAA 9 data\nwritten to the IDIN was nearly 94 percent, with a minimum of 89 percent\nin November\nand a maximum\nof\n96 percent\nin August.\nThe monthly\naverage\nrate of\nNOAA 9 data archived to the S-7 was 85 percent, with a\nminimum\nof 75 percent\nin January\nand a maximum\nof\n91 percent in October. The monthly average rate of data\nfrom the ERBE instrument\naboard the NOAA 10 spacecraft written to the ID1N was 97 percent. The minimum\nrate for the ID1N was 94 percent in June, and the maximum was 98 percent in November.\nThe monthly average\nrate of NOAA 10 data archived\nto the S-7 was 92 percent, with a minimum of 88 percent in May and a maximum of 94 percent in November.\nThese percentages\ndo\nnot include days for which no data were archived.\nOperations\nDuring\nMeasurements\n\nNormal\n\nEarth-Viewing\n\nThe nonscanner\ninstruments\noperated\nin the nadir\n(Earth-viewing)\nelevation\nmode, and the Solar Monitor\nAssembly\n(SMA)\nshutters\nremained\noff during normal\noperation.\nThe detector and solar port heaters remained\non, but all other nonscanner\ninstrument\nheaters, including\nthe ones that control\noutput of the calibration\nsources,\nremained\noff. The temperatures\nof the heat sinks and\napertures\nof the Earth-viewing\ndetectors on all three nonscanner\ninstruments\nwere controlled\nto nearly constant\nvalues during normal operation.\nCalibrations\nInternal\n\nand\n\nsolar\n\ncalibrations\n\nof\n\nthe\n\nnonscanner\n\ninstrument\non the ERBS spacecraft\nwere generally\nperformed every other Wednesday\nduring the period of this\npaper. The normal calibration\nschedule\nfor the instru-\n\nment on the ERBS spacecraft\nwas altered during full-Sun\nperiods.\nDuring these periods regularly\nscheduled\ncalibrations were not performed.\nInstead, a set of calibrations\nwas normally\nperformed\nimmediately\nprior to and after\nthe full-Sun periods. Internal and solar calibrations\nof the\nnonscanner\ninstruments\non the NOAA spacecraft\nwere\ngenerally\nperformed\non every Wednesday\nduring\nthe\nperiod of this paper. Beginning\nin June 1990, solar measurements\nwere performed\nevery Monday\nand Friday on\nthe NOAA 9 spacecraft.\nDuring\n1990, 30 successful\ninternal and solar calibrations were performed\non the ERBE nonscanner\ninstrument aboard the ERBS spacecraft.\nDuring 1990, 42 successful\ninternal calibrations\nand\n33 successful\nsolar calibrations\nwere performed\non the\nERBE nonscanner\ninstrument\naboard the NOAA 9 spacecraft. As with the instrument\non the ERBS spacecraft,\nalmost all the calibrations\nattempted\nwere successful.\nData from four nonscanner\nsolar calibrations\nand three\nnonscanner\ninternal\ncalibrations\nwere lost because\nof\ndata dropouts.\nOne nonscanner\nsolar calibration\nwas\nunsuccessful\nbecause\nof blockage\nof all four Earthviewing\nchannels\nand the solar monitor\nchannel\nat the\ntime of the Sun look. Because\nof the extremely\nlow 13\nangle, solar calibrations\nin October and November\nwere\nnot successful;\nduring this time, the Sun was below the\nspacecraft\nhorizon. A new calibration\nsequence\nthat left\nthe azimuth beam at 180 \xc2\xb0 was implemented\nin September\n1990. No internal\ncalibrations\nwere\nperformed\nbetween September\n12 and October 24, and no solar calibrations\nwere performed\nbetween\nSeptember\n12 and\nDecember\n12, 1990.\nDuring the period of this paper, there were 50 successful internal calibrations\nand 48 successful\nsolar calibrations of the ERBE nonscanner\ninstrument\naboard the\nNOAA\n10 spacecraft.\nOne of the internal\ncalibrations\nwas unsuccessful\ndue to data dropouts\nobscuring\nthe\ndata. Also, two solar calibrations\nwere obscured\nby data\ndropouts.\nOne of the solar calibrations\nwas unsuccessful\nbecause of suspect data.\nSolar Environment\nand Its Effect\nand Operation\nof Instruments\n\non the Response\n\nThe precession\nrate of the ERBS orbit produces\nvariations in the I_ angle from 10 \xc2\xb0 to 170 \xc2\xb0 over a 72-day\nperiod. The orbit plane crosses the Sun about every 36\ndays, and the spacecraft\nis in full-Sun orbits near the two\nextremes\nof I_. Solar heating\nincreases\nduring the fullSun periods,\nand housekeeping\ntemperature\nmeasurements on the instrument\nincrease significantly.\nWhen the\norbit plane crosses\nthe Sun, the ERBS spacecraft\nis\nyawed 180 \xc2\xb0 about the nadir axis to reposition\nthe solar\npanels to tilt to the Sun side of the orbit.\n21\n\nTheprecession of\nrate\nNOAA\n\n9 orbit continues\n\nthe ascending\nto increase\nand\n\nnode of the\nto make the\n\norbit less Sun synchronous.\nThis precession\nrate has\ncaused the local time of the ascending\nnode of the orbit\nto increase by 49 minutes between January\n1, 1989 and\nJanuary\n1, 1990. The 13 angle varied from 1.1 \xc2\xb0 to 33.4 \xc2\xb0\nduring the period of this paper. The spacecraft\nwas in full\nSun from January\n1 until May 20 and again from July 24\nthrough the end of the year. Due to the extremely\nlow [3\nangles toward the end of the year, the Sun was below the\nhorizon of the spacecraft,\nand hence not visible to the\ndetectors.\nNo solar calibrations\ncan be performed\nwhen 13\nis less than\n12 \xc2\xb0 . A new calibration\nsequence\nwas\nimplemented\nin October\n1990 that\nmuth rotations for solar calibrations.\n\ndid not include\n\nazi-\n\norder to obtain additional\nsolar\nERBE nonscanner\nsolar monitor\n\nAnomalies\nElevation\n\nin Operation\nBeams\n\nof Azimuth\n\nThe authors wish to thank\nence Applications\nInternational\nhis assistance with the tables.\n\nRobert Staples from SciCorporation\n(SAIC) for\n\nNASA Langley Research Center\nHampton, VA 23681-2199\nMarch 10, 1997\n\nReferences\nWeaver, William L.; Bush, Kathryn A.; Harris, Chris J.;\nHowerton, Clayton E.; and Tolson, Carol J.: Mission Description and In-Flight Operations of ERBE Instruments on ERBS\nand NOAA 9 Spacecraft--November\n1984-January\n1986.\nNASA RP-1256, 1991.\n2.\n\nand\n3.\n\nNo anomalies\nin azimuth or elevation\nbeam operation occurred\nin 1990.
